    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer749700458697856735">‘Locked-in syndrome: paralysed from head to toe, the patient, his mind intact, is imprisoned inside his own body, unable to speak or move. In my case, blinking my left eyelid is my only means of communication.’<br/><br/>In December 1995, Jean-Dominique Bauby, editor-in-chief of French ‘Elle’ and the father of two young children, suffered a massive stroke and found himself paralysed </span>
  <span id="freeText749700458697856735" style="display:none">‘Locked-in syndrome: paralysed from head to toe, the patient, his mind intact, is imprisoned inside his own body, unable to speak or move. In my case, blinking my left eyelid is my only means of communication.’<br /><br />In December 1995, Jean-Dominique Bauby, editor-in-chief of French ‘Elle’ and the father of two young children, suffered a massive stroke and found himself paralysed and speechless, but entirely conscious, trapped by what doctors call ‘locked-in syndrome’. Using his only functioning muscle – his left eyelid – he began dictating this remarkable story, painstakingly spelling it out letter by letter.<br /><br />His book offers a haunting, harrowing look inside the cruel prison of locked-in syndrome, but it is also a triumph of the human spirit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="749700458697856735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/331054-what-do-you-think-about-this-following">What do you think about this following passage ? What is he telling the reader? “I am fading away. Slowly but surely. Like the sailor who watches his home shore gradually disappear, I watch my past recede. My old life still burns within me, but more and more of it is reduced to the ashes of memory.” Pg 81</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_331054'><a id="like_count_community_question_331054" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/331054?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F193755.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/331054-what-do-you-think-about-this-following">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/331054-what-do-you-think-about-this-following">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/30620274-seamus"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1533273105i/30620274._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/30620274-seamus">Seamus</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">A person mostly sustains themselves with the constructions they have built of themselves, their place in the world and their life more generally. Baub<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">A person mostly sustains themselves with the constructions they have built of themselves, their place in the world and their life more generally. Bauby is talking about his present moment attachment to all those constructions and how his attachment to them and identification with them is fading. I don't use the word 'constructions' in a pejorative sense.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10369397395224835856">Words flow like the images and emotions of poetry. One thought leads to another. The rambling mind touches like a butterfly, just long enough to draw the essence from a story, and then moves fluidly to another. Jean-Dominique Bauby's body was an immobile weighty shell; the diving bell his perfect simile. Yet in his head he roved the world and composed the words that would let us in. Intent on looking for the cure to let him move again, he moves forward in his final words "We must keep looking. I</span>
  <span id="freeText10369397395224835856" style="display:none">Words flow like the images and emotions of poetry. One thought leads to another. Jean-Dominique Bauby's body was an immobile weighty shell; the diving bell his perfect simile. Yet in his head he roved the world and composed the words that would let us in. Intent on looking for the cure to let him move again, he moves forward in his final words "We must keep looking. I'll be off now", and six months later he was dead.<br /><br /><br /></span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19345798?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19345798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6922746421934717756">I know I will likely get flayed alive for rating this one so low, but I just can't see the worship behind it...<br/><br/>First, let me say that the "writing" of the book by someone in such a state is an amazing accomplishment and I dare not take that away from him. (For those that don't know, it was dictated by Jean-Dominique Bauby - former editor of the french Elle - who had a severe seizure and after damage to his brain stem, was diagnosed with locked in syndrome. The entire book was dictated, letter by</span>
  <span id="freeText6922746421934717756" style="display:none">I know I will likely get flayed alive for rating this one so low, but I just can't see the worship behind it...<br /><br />First, let me say that the "writing" of the book by someone in such a state is an amazing accomplishment and I dare not take that away from him. (For those that don't know, it was dictated by Jean-Dominique Bauby - former editor of the french Elle - who had a severe seizure and after damage to his brain stem, was diagnosed with locked in syndrome. The entire book was dictated, letter by letter, by the blinking of his left eye.)<br /><br />That said, you would think a memoir like this would at least try to present you with a likeable character who has something to say about life. Now, there were a couple of wistful, almost beautiful passages. But for the most part, I felt a sort of smugness in his attitude (especially towards women) that just pulled me out of any feelings the book tried to well up in me.<br /><br />Maybe I am a terrible person, but I just couldn't see why anyone would be so impressed by this book. Any time he would start to reel me in, one of his thoughts would throw me right out to sea again.<br /></span>

  <span id="freeText8316048516038523700" style="display:none">Prognosis: Man may be inspired &amp; find beauty even at his own death bed.<br /><br />But there is a question even Bauby asks himself: Does all of this a novel make?<br /><br />No. (Not even a decent... anecdote?)<br /><br />It is, however, testament of the prognosis which questions the central Meaning of Life question. Bauby finds personal beauty, even if he cannot do anything with it but blink it in code to his nurse since he is absolutely paralyzed. <br /><br />But this is no Anne Frank, however. This is no beauty pertaining to a person trapped. I feel some (but not complete) pity for Mr. Bauby. The tale this man tells is one of robbed mobility, but not of robbed dignity... or money. It bothered me a little that the editor of French Elle magazine, rich and powerful as he was, still alludes to and rubs in our faces his superiority, his nouveau riche lifestyle (cars, trips, experiences...). Even though it did not answer the central What is Life About? question, it did made me ponder something perhaps even more interesting: Can a healthy individual truly envy a "vegetable?" R.I.P.</span>

<div id="review_20886384" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20886384" />
    <a title="Gregory Baird" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1067337-gregory-baird"><img alt="Gregory Baird" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515353740p2/1067337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20886384?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gregory Baird" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gregory Baird" href="/user/show/1067337-gregory-baird">Gregory Baird</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=illness">illness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=adapted-for-film">adapted-for-film</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20886384" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18176887403300709213"><b> “Does it take the harsh light of disaster to show a person’s true nature?” </b><br/><br/>The situation is unimaginable: waking from a coma to find yourself trapped in your own body, able to think clearly and understand what is going on around you, but unable to partake in any of what transpires. It’s called “locked in syndrome,” and Jean-Dominique Bauby finds himself a victim of it when he awakes from a coma following a serious stroke that damaged his brain stem and left him almost totally paralyzed; he has </span>
  <span id="freeText18176887403300709213" style="display:none"><b> “Does it take the harsh light of disaster to show a person’s true nature?” </b><br /><br />The situation is unimaginable: waking from a coma to find yourself trapped in your own body, able to think clearly and understand what is going on around you, but unable to partake in any of what transpires. It’s called “locked in syndrome,” and Jean-Dominique Bauby finds himself a victim of it when he awakes from a coma following a serious stroke that damaged his brain stem and left him almost totally paralyzed; he has only limited facial movements, slight control over his neck, and use of only one eye. It is with this single good eye that Bauby is able to communicate with the world, using an excruciatingly slow code of blinking that requires time, energy, and a great deal of attention and patience. And it is also thanks to this one eye that we have this first-hand account, dictated by Bauby from his hospital bed, recounting the details of his life in the wake of tragedy.<br /><br />Far from being restricted by his condition, Bauby unleashes the full force of his literary capabilities (which were quite estimable, considering that he was the editor of French Elle), leaving us with a wry, touching, and deeply affecting memoir that shines with descriptive flourishes and deep insights. His perspective in the wake of tragedy is awe-inspiring and leaves the reader with a deep respect for his fortitude; truly, this is a man I would have loved to have had an opportunity to have a conversation with, just to try and absorb a small degree of his wisdom and experience. “The Diving Bell and the Butterfly” has moments of regret, frustration, sadness and aching loneliness, but curiously absent are anger and self-righteousness. Bauby never curses his misfortune but focuses on getting by with the hand he was dealt. To read his memoir is to get to know a truly extraordinary man whose spirit refused to be crushed and whose mind and imagination allowed him to survive in the most constrained of circumstances. To say that “The Diving Bell and the Butterfly” is about the triumph of the human spirit is a sorry understatement, and does little to pay tribute to an amazing man.<br /><br />“The Diving Bell and the Butterfly” is a slight volume, to be sure, but it feels wrong to criticize it for that when one considers the conditions under which it was composed. And considering that Bauby packs a hefty punch in such a short page count, it is well worth the experience.<br /><br />Grade: A-</span>
  <a data-text-id="18176887403300709213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35744813" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35744813?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=all-time-favorites-non-fiction">all-time-favorites-non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=autobiography">autobiography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35744813" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13213217982446287360">Jean-Dominique Baube, the forty-something editor of Elle magazine in Paris, husband, father, was stricken by a rare brain disease. After several weeks in a coma he awoke to find that he was a prisoner inside his own body, with control over only his left eye, and motion limited to twisting his head left and right, somewhat. Yet this man managed, with help, to not only maintain his sanity and his optimism, but his appreciation of beauty and his sense of humor. This is a case in which imagination i</span>
  <span id="freeText13213217982446287360" style="display:none">Jean-Dominique Baube, the forty-something editor of Elle magazine in Paris, husband, father, was stricken by a rare brain disease. After several weeks in a coma he awoke to find that he was a prisoner inside his own body, with control over only his left eye, and motion limited to twisting his head left and right, somewhat. Yet this man managed, with help, to not only maintain his sanity and his optimism, but his appreciation of beauty and his sense of humor. This is a case in which imagination is a critical tool to one’s very survival. The guy wrote a book using little more than his left eye blinking code to an interpreter. This is not at all a depressing memoir. It is inspiring. In fact it is one of the most positive, uplifting things I have ever read. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12470816287856306997">**spoiler alert**<br/><br/>In 1995, Jean-Dominique Bauby, the editor-in-chief of French Elle magazine, suffered a massive stroke to his brain stem which left him totally paralyzed and in a condition called locked-in syndrome.   <span id="freeText12470816287856306997" style="display:none">**spoiler alert**<br /><br />In 1995, Jean-Dominique Bauby, the editor-in-chief of French Elle magazine, suffered a massive stroke to his brain stem which left him totally paralyzed and in a condition called locked-in syndrome. He could only move his left eyelid. For my part, I would have preferred to have died instantly than to have suffered what Mr. Bauby suffered. But we don't always get to choose, and to his credit he seems to have made the best of his situation. He did write this book after all, but only by dictating it one letter at a time by blinking his left eyelid. You can find inspiration in this book, you can find beauty; it just doesn't mask the tragedy and the suffering.</span>

<div id="review_1278333102" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1278333102" />
    <a title="Jasmine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21803154-jasmine"><img alt="Jasmine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582366251p2/21803154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1278333102?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jasmine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jasmine" href="/user/show/21803154-jasmine">Jasmine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Definitely Everyone.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=movie-based">movie-based</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=realism">realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=will-reread">will-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=beautiful-stories">beautiful-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=highly-recommend">highly-recommend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=preciously-owned">preciously-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=thought-provoking">thought-provoking</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=touching">touching</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21803154-jasmine?shelf=light-read">light-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1278333102" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer790959970244576658">This book involves 28 short stories, or you can say, pieces of memory from the former editor of French <i>Elle</i> magazine, Jean-Dominique Bauby, who was permanently paralyzed after a severe stroke. His only way of communication was by blinking his left eye and that was how he patiently spelled this book out. As he put it, and I firmly believed in him, that his main task was to <b>
  <i>
    <u>"compose the first of these bedridden travel notes so that I shall be ready when my publisher's emissary arrives to t</u></i></b></span>
  <span id="freeText790959970244576658" style="display:none">This book involves 28 short stories, or you can say, pieces of memory from the former editor of French <i>Elle</i> magazine, Jean-Dominique Bauby, who was permanently paralyzed after a severe stroke. His only way of communication was by blinking his left eye and that was how he patiently spelled this book out. As he put it, and I firmly believed in him, that his main task was to <b>
  <i>
    <u>"compose the first of these bedridden travel notes so that I shall be ready when my publisher's emissary arrives to take my dictation, letter by letter. In my head I churn over every sentence ten times, delete a word, add an adjective, and learn my text by heart, paragraph by paragraph."</u>
  </i>
</b><br><br>There was no particular order for the topics in this book, nor was there any certain connection among them. What made it precious for me is how detailed Jean-Dominique depicted of what he saw, what he heard(despite his serious hearing disorder), and most important of all, what he felt. He was suddenly forced to embrace his "new life" after the misfortune, but it's rather impressive that he didn't think so sometimes. <br><br>Although I could feel the helplessness in his voice and the eagerness of freedom when he was comfined to an unfamiliar wheelchair--just like a <b>diving bell</b> waiting to be opened up--there were times he really enjoyed himself in spite of his disability. In a nutshell, he chose to be a carefree <b>butterfly</b>, making his spirit live on forever without burden and pain. What's more, this book/his words made me <b>realize how blissful I am because I can live out loud, do whatever I want, go wherever I desire, talk and hang out with friends/family whenever we're available...etc</b>. Therefore, he kind of reminded us to know and cherish such blessing since we never know how precious it is until we lose it. <br><br>Lastly, <i>The Diving Bell and the Butterfly</i> is definitely a must-read in life and I highly recommend it to everyone. The original version is in French, <i>Le Scaphandre et le Papillon</i>, and I found the translation one captured his meanings pretty well. By the way, I can't put an end to this review without quoting something worth valuing, so here it is: <blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"Capturing the moment, these small slices of life, these small gusts of happiness, move me more deeply than all the rest."</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1456672287i/18265222._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="790959970244576658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_902732067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/902732067" />
    <a title="Cassy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2282938-cassy"><img alt="Cassy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565892954p2/2282938.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/902732067?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cassy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cassy" href="/user/show/2282938-cassy">Cassy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those seeking the perfect balance of sadness and beauty</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cassy by: </span>
      The movie, which I never watched but cost me $60 in Netflix fees
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2282938-cassy?shelf=physical-own">physical-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2282938-cassy?shelf=non-fiction-biography">non-fiction-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2282938-cassy?shelf=region-europe">region-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2282938-cassy?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2282938-cassy?shelf=acquired-2014">acquired-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2282938-cassy?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer902732067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9001865300339464339">I won’t recommend reading this book while signing up for insurance. I started a job recently and was overwhelmed by the different ways I could insure myself and loved ones against horrible tidings. [The following are actual insurance plans. I couldn’t make these up.]<br/><blockquote>Benefits Department: Do you want life insurance?<br/><br/>Me: Yes! Someone should profit from my death. Party at the funeral home!<br/><br/>Benefits Department: Do you want supplement group variable universal life insurance?<br/><br/>Me: I could be worth $2.5 mil</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9001865300339464339" style="display:none">I won’t recommend reading this book while signing up for insurance. I started a job recently and was overwhelmed by the different ways I could insure myself and loved ones against horrible tidings. [The following are actual insurance plans. I couldn’t make these up.]<br /><blockquote>Benefits Department: Do you want life insurance?<br /><br />Me: Yes! Someone should profit from my death. Party at the funeral home!<br /><br />Benefits Department: Do you want supplement group variable universal life insurance?<br /><br />Me: I could be worth $2.5 million? Tell my parents! I am a wild success!<br /><br />Benefits Department: Do you want life insurance for your spouse?<br /><br />Me: I hadn’t thought of that. Yes! I will definitely miss the cash flow from my sugar daddy once the arsenic kicks in. <br /><br />Benefits Department: Do you want life insurance for your child?<br /><br />Me: What a sick question. Why would I care about cash if my child has just died?<br /><br />Benefits Department: For the funeral expenses.<br /><br />Me: Oh, okay. Yes.<br /><br />Benefits Department: Do you want to enroll in the group short-term disability insurance? Group income insurance? Accidental death and dismemberment insurance? Long term care insurance? Group personal excess liability insurance? Business travel accident insurance?<br /><br />Me: OMG. I have never felt so fragile. I could die or, at least, lose a limb in a thousand different ways. Did you hear about the guy who was decapitated by the elevator doors? Blood and gore splattered all over the other passengers!<br /><br />Benefits Department: [Silence]<br /><br />Me: Everything. Sign me up for everything. After all, I ride at least four elevators a day to work. [Imagine I am really good at mental math here.] That’s 1,040 possibilities each year to lose my mind, literally. Just from elevators!<br /><br />Benefits Department: [Pause] I need you to confirm “yes” or “no” for each type of insurance.<br /><br />Me: Yes. Yes. Yes. Yes. Yes. Yes. <br /><br />Benefits Department: Do you want to prepare a health care proxy to instruct your loved ones how to care for you when you are too sick to speak and/or have no control over very important medical decisions about life support or pain treatment?<br /><br />Me: You are the worst, insurance person. Do you derive twisted pleasure picturing me like that? <br /><br />Benefits Department: [Smug silence]<br /><br />Me: Yes. I would like a health care proxy. <br /><br />Benefits Department: Do you want to sign up for…</blockquote>By the eventual end of this conversation, the Benefits Department informed me that several hundred dollars would be deducted from every pay check to cover these nefarious insurance schemes preying simultaneously on my fear of dying, concern for my family, general risk aversion, and fondness for the mere possibility of large sums of money being transferred to my bank account. I called my husband to discuss my selections. He was aghast. <br /><blockquote>Husband: Cassy, this seems excessive. We don’t need this much coverage. We need some money left over in your pay check to buy food. Or we will indeed die early. Of hunger.<br /><br />Me: But what if I die? What if you die? What if the baby dies? What if I’m paralyzed after a massive stroke in my forties. I cannot move anything except I can blink my left eyelid. While people treat me like I’m an invisible, vapid vegetable, my mind is as sharp as ever. I could write the most beautiful, poignant memoir packed into a concise 132 pages by blinking that one good eye to select letters when the alphabet is read aloud to me over and over by a very patient soul. What then, husband?</blockquote>Then it hit me. This book. It should not be read while one signs up for insurance. It lingers in the back of the mind. This could happen to me. Or to you. This happened to Jean-Dominique Bauby. Read this. Not during benefits enrollment. But read this.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9001865300339464339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12746051177114507960"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/193755.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly' title='The Diving Bell and the Butterfly by Jean-Dominique Bauby' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Diving Bell and the Butterfly</a><br/><br/>Locked-in-syndrome: totally paralyzed, unable to speak, but completely conscious. <br/>I find it hard to imagine a condition that's worse than this one. <br/>People who suffer a stroke, are at a risk to suffer from this condition (luckily, mostly not this bad). <br/>Is there still dignity in a life like this? <br/><br/>The writer of this memoir, suffered from this condition, and was only able to move one eye. His left eye. <br/>Needless to say this was a powerful read. Its popularity is partl</span>
  <span id="freeText12746051177114507960" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/193755.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly" title="The Diving Bell and the Butterfly by Jean-Dominique Bauby" rel="nofollow noopener">The Diving Bell and the Butterfly</a><br /><br />Locked-in-syndrome: totally paralyzed, unable to speak, but completely conscious. <br />I find it hard to imagine a condition that's worse than this one. <br />People who suffer a stroke, are at a risk to suffer from this condition (luckily, mostly not this bad). <br />Is there still dignity in a life like this? <br /><br />The writer of this memoir, suffered from this condition, and was only able to move one eye. His left eye. <br />Needless to say this was a powerful read. Its popularity is partly due to the fact that this book was made into a successful movie. <br /><br />However, there's a similar memoir that is not as well known as this one, and which I thought was much better and more powerful : <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19784430.Only_The_Eyes_Say_Yes_A_Love_Story" title="Only The Eyes Say Yes A Love Story by Philippe Vigand" rel="nofollow noopener">Only The Eyes Say Yes: A Love Story</a>. <br />If you liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/193755.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly" title="The Diving Bell and the Butterfly by Jean-Dominique Bauby" rel="nofollow noopener">The Diving Bell and the Butterfly</a>, I highly recommend <i>Only the eyes say yes</i>.</span>

<div id="review_400174002" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400174002" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1170391-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590218363p2/1170391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400174002?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/1170391-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307389251" href="/book/show/1878692.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=library-loan">library-loan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=biographies-and-memoirs">biographies-and-memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400174002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4141918203894337298">After suffering a catastrophic cerebro-vascular stroke, Jean-Dominique Bauby wakes up with a rare condition known as ‘locked in’ syndrome. Reduced to only being able to move his left eyelid to communicate, he takes us through his days following the event and his struggle to regain his identity. <br/><br/>I’ve wanted to read this for a long time, but have never felt in the right frame of mind to read it. I work within neurosciences, and a lot of our patients have either had strokes or have some other form </span>
  <span id="freeText4141918203894337298" style="display:none">After suffering a catastrophic cerebro-vascular stroke, Jean-Dominique Bauby wakes up with a rare condition known as ‘locked in’ syndrome. Reduced to only being able to move his left eyelid to communicate, he takes us through his days following the event and his struggle to regain his identity. <br /><br />I’ve wanted to read this for a long time, but have never felt in the right frame of mind to read it. I work within neurosciences, and a lot of our patients have either had strokes or have some other form of neurological condition, and I wanted to read this in order to understand a bit more about the experiences these individuals go through and the (often harrowing) range of emotions this can encompass. <br /><br />It’s a short read, as Jean-Dominique transcends simply relating his time in hospital and his attempts at recovery. This is about trying to rediscover identity in an individual who has lost all of himself. Without the ability to communicate, he breaks down recounting that he can’t ruffle his son’s hair on a whim anymore, and he lives for the scent of fried food to help him remember fond memories. At times he describes his dreams, with an almost stream of conscious approach that I found both deeply moving at times, but also jarring against the harsh reality of his current life. It’s so sad, I just can’t quite put into words how awful it must be to live an almost ‘half life’ like this, where sounds and memories are warped to the point where it’s difficult to remember what’s real and what isn’t anymore. <br /><br />I rated this three stars, which means I enjoyed it, but I think the rating reflects my overall experience and feelings when I finished it. I don’t want to reread it, I appreciate and highly value what I have read, but my mood was so flat by the end. The bleakness is just made worse by the realness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4141918203894337298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18723268" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18723268" />
    <a title="Yulia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/185835-yulia"><img alt="Yulia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508793572p2/185835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18723268?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yulia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yulia" href="/user/show/185835-yulia">Yulia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780375401152" href="/book/show/1201140.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/185835-yulia?shelf=me-moirs">me-moirs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18723268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14190714079776261062">I just saw the movie adaptation last Friday, the day before my father-in-law passed away: perhaps not the best time, but I'm the type of person who refuses to get myself out of my mood, but prefers to dwell on my feelings. I actually found the movie much darker than the book itself, which I read when it first came out in English. As the book's from his perspective, we are spared the experience of the silence and loneliness he is encased in. The movie, in contrast, depicts just how terrifying and</span>
  <span id="freeText14190714079776261062" style="display:none">I just saw the movie adaptation last Friday, the day before my father-in-law passed away: perhaps not the best time, but I'm the type of person who refuses to get myself out of my mood, but prefers to dwell on my feelings. I actually found the movie much darker than the book itself, which I read when it first came out in English. As the book's from his perspective, we are spared the experience of the silence and loneliness he is encased in. The movie, in contrast, depicts just how terrifying and isolating locked-in syndrome is, how claustrophobic and powerless the person who is afflicted becomes despite no deficit in cognitive ability, how he must have yelled inside only to appear silent and unresponsive. Truly a living hell. So it was interesting to compare the vivid, active, articulate world he presents us with in his book with the walls he faced in connecting with others who simply lacked the patience to "listen" to him and see he was still so much present. <br /><br />Perhaps the most powerful contrast in the movie, which I don't remember but may be depicted in the book, is the polar responses of the mother of his children, who visited him almost every week and loved him despite all the pain he'd caused her, and his lover just before his stroke, who never had the courage to visit him in his enfeebled state, but said she was always "with him" in spirit, though he was able to communicate to her, "chaque jour j'attends": each day I wait. I knew then that I would choose to be the kind of person who was there for those I loved despite my fears and stress, not the one who was there in spirit but not in person. The lover was a coward. Her selfish desire to cling to the healthy Bauby was, for me, inexcusable. Se behaved as if he'd already died, when this book clearly shows he was perhaps never more lucid about his connection to the world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14190714079776261062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31044270" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31044270" />
    <a title="Laurel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/957217-laurel"><img alt="Laurel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576972516p2/957217.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31044270?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laurel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laurel" href="/user/show/957217-laurel">Laurel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio Cassette 9780679460848" href="/book/show/2207750.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/957217-laurel?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/957217-laurel?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31044270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4511864829387464901">This is one of those books where the story behind the story was more interesting to me than the book itself. For those who don't know, Jean-Dominique Bauby (the former editor-in-chief of French <i>Elle</i>) had a stroke in his mid 40s that left him with a body entirely paralyzed but a mind fully intact. This is referred to as locked-in syndrome, a condition that, in the author's words, is "like a mind in a jar."<br/><br/>The one part of his body that Bauby could move was his left eyelid. Remarkably, he was able </span>
  <span id="freeText4511864829387464901" style="display:none">This is one of those books where the story behind the story was more interesting to me than the book itself. For those who don't know, Jean-Dominique Bauby (the former editor-in-chief of French <i>Elle</i>) had a stroke in his mid 40s that left him with a body entirely paralyzed but a mind fully intact. This is referred to as locked-in syndrome, a condition that, in the author's words, is "like a mind in a jar."<br /><br />The one part of his body that Bauby could move was his left eyelid. Remarkably, he was able to use this single movement to communicate. As a special alphabet was dictated to him, Bauby blinked when he heard the letter he wanted to convey. In this way, slowly and patiently, letters became words, then sentences, and ultimately ended in this memoir. <br /><br />This in and of itself makes me want to give his book a 5 star rating, as the fact it is even in existence is extraordinary. But, while the book is well-written, there just lacked a sense of real introspection that I had been expecting. It left me wishing he had let us in to his thoughts a bit more deeply than he did. Still, absolutely worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4511864829387464901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

A remarkable story of a remarkable man, so full of life one minute and reduced to movement in one eye the next, a haunting, harrowing look inside the mind of a person with locked-in syndrome you would think this would end up as a rant of anger and "why did this happen to me", but his dignified manner and spirit was truly inspirational and caught me out at just how uplifting it would turn ou</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17099846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11393987683930558174">this is quite an achievement .<br/>the writer a quadraplegic with locked in syndrome who died shortly after the publication of this book mangages to make himself into a totally unsympathetic character .<br/>i really disliked this book i suppose because it has gained such an good reputation . this is mis-lit at its worst . <br/>the author is completely self obsessed perhaps unsurprisingly and the profundity is not much above that found in a Hallmark card . it seems that the things the author misses most are his</span>
  <span id="freeText11393987683930558174" style="display:none">this is quite an achievement .<br />the writer a quadraplegic with locked in syndrome who died shortly after the publication of this book mangages to make himself into a totally unsympathetic character .<br />i really disliked this book i suppose because it has gained such an good reputation . this is mis-lit at its worst . <br />the author is completely self obsessed perhaps unsurprisingly and the profundity is not much above that found in a Hallmark card . it seems that the things the author misses most are his trips to Hong Kong , his BMW , his phone calls to important people and generally the go-go life of a magazine editor . one of the worst chapters is when he describes his selfishness towards his girlfriend or wife and manages to turn it into a piece of self glorification . she loves him anyway because he is such a brilliant and incorrible man . well so he thinks . the smugness is unbelievable .<br />it is all very pollyanerish and trite really . his realationship with his children and their mother seems superficial and distant .<br />with the recent revelations about false memoirs in this mis-lit genre i am very suspicious as to how much of the writer's story is true and how much of it was written by someone else . he supposedly set up an organisation to support those with locked in syndrome , just as Bob Geldof has supposedly saved the world .<br />the Guardian said " everyone in the country should own at least one copy " . why would anyone want more than one copy . I only own one copy of " The waste land " , so why should i or anyone buy 2 copies of this rubbish ?<br />Like many books the marketing story is more important than the book itself .<br />Harsh but fair ! <br /></span>

<div id="review_3181425913" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3181425913" />
    <a title="Katy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41454801-katy"><img alt="Katy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558302131p2/41454801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3181425913?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katy" href="/user/show/41454801-katy">Katy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780375401152" href="/book/show/1201140.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41454801-katy?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41454801-katy?shelf=memoir-biography">memoir-biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3181425913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15409863322009764086">What an literary treasure! The utter sadness of this author’s condition is magnificently juxtaposed to this heart warming memoir. Jean-Do, as his loved ones called him, has written an unbelievably upbeat and often humorous recollection of memorable events revisited during his most devastating period of life.<br/><br/>Once the editor in chief of Elle magazine, and a very high profile socialite in Paris, his magnificent mind is now relegated to a whirlwind of thoughts and anecdotes told in a most painfully </span>
  <span id="freeText15409863322009764086" style="display:none">What an literary treasure! The utter sadness of this author’s condition is magnificently juxtaposed to this heart warming memoir. Jean-Do, as his loved ones called him, has written an unbelievably upbeat and often humorous recollection of memorable events revisited during his most devastating period of life.<br /><br />Once the editor in chief of Elle magazine, and a very high profile socialite in Paris, his magnificent mind is now relegated to a whirlwind of thoughts and anecdotes told in a most painfully slow episode of one letter at a time.<br /><br />It is quite amazing how positively expressed he is given the untimely tragedy that has overtaken his life. That, I suppose, is his survival instinct.<br /><br />At the age of 43, Jean-Do suffers a massive stroke that renders him entirely paralyzed from the neck to his toes, left in a condition called Locked-in syndrome. He is unable to speak or eat, and while he can turn his head, his only other ability is to be able to blink one eye. He uses that ability to communicate his memoir, dictating one individual letter of each word at a time, having previously thought out and memorized each chapter.<br /><br />The writing is very engaging and enjoyable, exquisite actually. This man truly has astounding literary talents beyond his editorial skills. Most of the stories take place at the time after his stroke, so he is reflecting upon his motionless life in a wheelchair in a medical facility. His courage and love for life and his family is quite evident. His recognition of a life cut short and opportunities missed is truly heartbreaking.<br /><br />Living each and everyday to its fullest is so very important, and recognizing life’s fragility is inevitable.<br /><br />A short but very moving story that is both sad and uplifting at the same time. Although this book has been translated from French, it flows beautifully without any indications of “lost in the translation “. Ingenious!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15409863322009764086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1"><img alt="Shovelmonkey1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391713268p2/3926982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307389251" href="/book/show/1878692.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who thinks they might be in danger of taking life for granted</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Shovelmonkey1 by: </span>
      Jon
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=bookcrossing-books">bookcrossing-books</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer184309402" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer768007781314318893">When I first heard about it I did not think it would be the sort of thing I would be interested in reading and definately not the sort of thing I would be interested in watching (having heard it had recently been turned into a film). One of my best friends, a man who is a great deal more sensitive and open minded than I could ever hope to be asked me if i'd read it. "A book about a man who wrote the book by blinking one eyelid?" I asked. "But I like the Die Hard Quadrology, 300 and Wilbur Smith </span>
  <span id="freeText768007781314318893" style="display:none">When I first heard about it I did not think it would be the sort of thing I would be interested in reading and definately not the sort of thing I would be interested in watching (having heard it had recently been turned into a film). One of my best friends, a man who is a great deal more sensitive and open minded than I could ever hope to be asked me if i'd read it. "A book about a man who wrote the book by blinking one eyelid?" I asked. "But I like the Die Hard Quadrology, 300 and Wilbur Smith novels - where do you think this book is going to fit into my world view? It won't fit! It will make me feel bad for hating it too" Anyway after a raised eye brow from my much less judgemental friend and with a view to being open minded I gave it a go....<br /><br />Taaa-dah!<br />Open minded enlightenment achieved. This book was interesting and easy to read although if you are feeling a bit depressed then I would suggest avoiding it. Although that said, it is unlikely your situation is going to be worse than that of Jean Dominique Bauby so maybe it'll help you get a little perspective and stop you being such a sad sack.<br /><br />The lucid way in which Jean-dominique describes what it feels like to be locked into his own body is amazing and the tireless descriptions are even more amazing when you consider how each word made it on to the page. Heartfelt, unique and inescapably sad. If you choose to read this book it will probably be the most unique thing you get to read this year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="768007781314318893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7343923901418619239" style="display:none">This was a difficult read with regards to subject matter, but not when it comes to writing style. Jean-Dominique Bauby, the former editor of the French edition of “Elle” magazine, was paralyzed after a huge stroke. It’s amazing to me that he dictated this book letter by letter by blinking one eyelid, the only part of his body that wasn’t paralyzed. The fact that he managed to keep his brain engaged and alive given his situation is a miracle.               >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13321740744772693802">I read it in an afternoon and it is, in one word, <em>beautiful</em>. <br/><br/>This book was, for me, about the simple things we often overlook, mostly because we're too busy and don't take the time to actually look, but also because seeing takes a particular state of mind, it requires an openness, that is not always easy to come by. <br/><br/>Jean-Dominique definitely had the time to observe the world, and the immensity of the strength, courage and determination it took to put a fraction of those things onto paper is enou</span>
  <span id="freeText13321740744772693802" style="display:none">I read it in an afternoon and it is, in one word, <em>beautiful</em>. <br /><br />This book was, for me, about the simple things we often overlook, mostly because we're too busy and don't take the time to actually look, but also because seeing takes a particular state of mind, it requires an openness, that is not always easy to come by. <br /><br />Jean-Dominique definitely had the time to observe the world, and the immensity of the strength, courage and determination it took to put a fraction of those things onto paper is enough to make this a worthwhile read. The book might seem pointless at times, but if you remember the state of Jean-Dominique you can appreciate why he might have chosen to write about those exact things. He wanted to say this, not so much for you to read it, but as a way to deal with his loss, a loss that confided him to a bed, paralyzed, while his mind still worked as well as ever. <br /><br />What he gives us is not a story, it is instead a string of thoughts, split into short chapters on various subjects. They’re sometimes rambling, sometimes profound, and always thoughts he couldn't get out any other way. They say actions speak louder than words, but sometimes all you have are words and if you use them right, they'll count for just as much.<br /><br />I can't help but think of this book as his goodbye and hello to the world. I don't think he wrote it for me, the reader, or anyone else, I believe he wrote it mostly for himself. For some reason that's what I like about it. That it isn't for the readers, yet we can still take part in it. I can see the world from his eyes, I can lie in that hospital bed and feel the heavy absence of feeling, living limbs. <br /><br />As he also states in the book, the only other person with this illness/condition in the history of literature is to be found in The Count of Monte Cristo. Regardless of anything else I could say, this is a unique book just because it's such a rare point of view. Don't miss it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13321740744772693802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3623121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3623121" />
    <a title="Aberjhani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/226404-aberjhani"><img alt="Aberjhani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442461494p2/226404.jpg" /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/226404-aberjhani?shelf=magazine">magazine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/226404-aberjhani?shelf=writing">writing</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3623121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1073436788038477699"><b>A Small Book with a Big Soul</b> <br/><br/>Jean-Dominique Bauby's "The Diving Bell and the Butterfly" is a small book composed of many big wonders. Primary among this book's extraordinary qualities is the fact that Bauby, a former editor in chief of the world-famous French Elle, was able to "write" it at all. after suffering a stroke to his brain stem and spending 20 days in a coma, Bauby regained command of a nearly clairvoyant intellect but lost all authority over his body. The sole physical function he ret</span>
  <span id="freeText1073436788038477699" style="display:none"><b>A Small Book with a Big Soul</b> <br /><br />Jean-Dominique Bauby's "The Diving Bell and the Butterfly" is a small book composed of many big wonders. Primary among this book's extraordinary qualities is the fact that Bauby, a former editor in chief of the world-famous French Elle, was able to "write" it at all. after suffering a stroke to his brain stem and spending 20 days in a coma, Bauby regained command of a nearly clairvoyant intellect but lost all authority over his body. The sole physical function he retained was the ability to blink his left eye; by use of it, he developed a kind of sign language that allowed him to dictate letter by painstaking letter the brief and luminous chapters that make up "The Diving Bell and the Butterfly."<br /><br />Anyone could easily have forgiven bauby had he chosen to lapse into the kind of rage and unhinged sentimentality that characterize (although justifiably so) other memoirs based on extreme medical situations. However, he takes a wholly different route. Like "the invisible diving bell" that imprisons his body and the butterfly wings of memory and meditation that provide some relief, Bauby's prose floats back and forth between the severe and the sublime. astonishing above all else is the stream of humor that flows unforced and unfettered throughout the book, as when the editor insists on being allowed to drool while dressed in cashmere rather than in hospital garb. <br /><br />From musings on the glamour of his former life to the simple pleasures of a leisurely bath, this book contains much irony and healthy doses of cynicism. It displays as well the brilliant dignity of one damaged soul's refusal to fade into nothingness before having its final say. Despite Bauby's death two days after the french publication of his book, his voice will boom through these pages for many years to come. <br /><br />Aberjhani<br />author of I Made My Boy Out of Poetry<br />and Encyclopedia of the Harlem Renaissance</span>
  <a data-text-id="1073436788038477699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44892929" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44892929" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6896-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1176778349p2/6896.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44892929?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/6896-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44892929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8419649790923431914">The power of this short memoir is that it exists.<br/><br/><i>The Diving Bell and the Butterfly</i> is a collection of short reflections and anecdotes by Jean-Dominique Bauby, the once-editor of the French <i>Elle</i> who at age forty-three suffered a massive stroke that left him completely paralyzed, unable to move or communicate save by blinking his left eye.<br/><br/>Knowing this, every page of prose is a modern miracle. It helps that the prose is really good.<br/><br/>The experience of reading this, then, becomes a source of great cog</span>
  <span id="freeText8419649790923431914" style="display:none">The power of this short memoir is that it exists.<br /><br /><i>The Diving Bell and the Butterfly</i> is a collection of short reflections and anecdotes by Jean-Dominique Bauby, the once-editor of the French <i>Elle</i> who at age forty-three suffered a massive stroke that left him completely paralyzed, unable to move or communicate save by blinking his left eye.<br /><br />Knowing this, every page of prose is a modern miracle. It helps that the prose is really good.<br /><br />The experience of reading this, then, becomes a source of great cognitive dissonance: how could such keen and lively writing come from such immobility? It is an example of a marvelous separation—or independence—of body and mind.<br /><br />This dissonance little resolves, however. The story’s ultimate tragedy looms on every page—in Bauby’s tone, in his fluctuation between thankfulness, hope and dread, and in the reactions of Bauby’s friends when they come to visit. Somehow, it seems the miracle can’t last, and that leaves us marveling at its existence even more.<br /><br /><i>Do I recommend it?</i> Yes, this is a short, exceptional read.<br /><i>Would I teach it?</i> Maybe. Bauby is wonderful with images.<br /><i>Lasting impression</i>: That Bauby “wrote” this is a testament to the capaciousness of the mind, and it is yet another reminder that personal appearance is never a true reflection of the life of the mind.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8419649790923431914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_357249611" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/357249611" />
    <a title="Peter Heinrich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3813664-peter-heinrich"><img alt="Peter Heinrich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275431437p2/3813664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/357249611?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Heinrich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Heinrich" href="/user/show/3813664-peter-heinrich">Peter Heinrich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307389251" href="/book/show/1878692.The_Diving_Bell_and_the_Butterfly">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813664-peter-heinrich?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813664-peter-heinrich?shelf=biography">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer357249611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16900096573621297900">The wide margins and short chapters of this thin volume are important physical features Bauby's memoir. They're a constant reminder that every word, every letter came at a very high cost. It's impossible to forget, if a passage seems short, that it had to be dictated one letter at a time, using an amazingly laborious process called <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partner_assisted_scanning' rel='nofollow noopener'>partner-assisted scanning</a>—an interlocutor recites the alphabet until the "speaker" indicates the correct letter has been reached, at which point the letter is copied </span>
  <span id="freeText16900096573621297900" style="display:none">The wide margins and short chapters of this thin volume are important physical features Bauby's memoir. They're a constant reminder that every word, every letter came at a very high cost. It's impossible to forget, if a passage seems short, that it had to be dictated one letter at a time, using an amazingly laborious process called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partner_assisted_scanning" rel="nofollow noopener">partner-assisted scanning</a>—an interlocutor recites the alphabet until the "speaker" indicates the correct letter has been reached, at which point the letter is copied down.<br><br>It amazes me that Bauby was able to compose and edit each chapter in his head, then dictate it from memory. It's obvious that Bauby absolutely had to write this book, whether or not it was ever published. It's an extended letter to his family and others close to him, and that's how I read it.<br><br>A geeky aside, since I'm a tech guy: Bauby didn't describe in detail the scanning method he employed with Claude Mendibil, but it sounds like a simple linear search. Even with the alphabet arranged in decreasing order of frequency, this is still <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation" rel="nofollow noopener"><em>O</em>(<em>n</em>)</a>. On <em>average</em>, it looks like you'd have to check ~12 letters before you found the right one.<br><br>Searching a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huffman_coding" rel="nofollow noopener">Huffman tree</a>, on the other hand, is only <em>O</em>(log <em>n</em>). Mendibil could have held up a chart depicting the alphabet Huffman-encoded into an optimal binary search tree for French <em>(click below for my version)</em>, then, starting at the root, pointed to each node. On a blink from Bauby, she would move down to the right, otherwise down to the left. Reaching a leaf would mean a letter had been selected. This would have meant more blinking for Bauby, but almost a 3× increase in dictation speed. On <em>average</em>, you'd only have to check ~4½ letters each time.<br><br><a href="https://dl.dropbox.com/u/1301108/Goodreads/huff_fr.png" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="French Huffman Tree" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380382196i/739928.png" width="400" height="221" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>[Based on letter frequencies from <a href="http://www.rosettagrams.com/letter_frequencies.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">Rosettagram</a>.]</i><br><br><br>Hey, I <em>said</em> it was geeky.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16900096573621297900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7527473659285940604" style="display:none"><a href="http://kiribear.blogspot.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Check out my blog to see Reviews of Book and Movies, and check out some Recipes!</a><br /><br />This was definitely a quick and interesting read. I have actually never seen the movie, and this was my first time getting around to reading the book. The write Jean-Dominique Bauby wrote with the aid of a "translator" that had to have taken an extensive amount of time. Jean-Dominique, former editor in chief of Elle magazine, becomes a quadriplegic after a stroke affects his brain stem. He can only communicate in a type of one eye blinking to make it through an altered alphabet. He suffers from what is known as "locked-in" syndrome in which the brain is still functioning at normal, but the body is not responding as it should. <br /><br />The fact that this book even exists at all given his condition is quite fascinating. Just the implications of what it took to write the book are insane. It was snippets of his current and past life woven together in what seems like a short novel to us, but undoubtedly was an immense amount of time and effort upon him and his "translator". <br /><br />A solid 4 stars, and worth reading, just for his effort of writing it.</span>
